        <div class="viewGalleryDescriptionHolder" itemprop="description">
         <div class="viewGalleryDescription " id="viewGalleryDescription">
          Paths are invitations to explore, to step out of your home and venture into nature
          <span>
           <span>
            <span>
             <span>
              
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
          even if its your own backyard.
          <span>
           <span>
            <span>
             <span>
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
          <span>
           <span>
            <span>
             <span>
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
          In fact, a thoughtfully designed path can turn a simple walk across the yard into an opportunity to slow down and notice your surroundings. From a stepping stone path leading through a shady woodland to a wide tree-lined path meant for walking hand in hand, here are 15 ideas for creating the garden path of your dreams.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="out" id="gallerySpaces" itemprop="articleBody" lb-context="gallery_45216766" scopeid="gallerySpaces">
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="4876124" objid="4876124" posid="0">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/4876124/Zen-Rock-Garden-asian-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>

            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/4876124/Zen-Rock-Garden-asian-landscape-other-metro">

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="4876124">
            <b>
             1. Rock-wall-lined path.
            </b>
            Low, stacked rock walls line the edges of this gently curving gravel path. Using stone walls creates raised planting beds alongside the path, allowing the plants to spill over without overtaking the walkway.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="3417531" objid="3417531" posid="1">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/3417531/Japanese-style-landscaping-traditional-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>
             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by dabah landscape designs" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'3417531');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/9a811b2a0159e310_1556-w606-h454-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/3417531/Japanese-style-landscaping-traditional-landscape-other-metro">
             <span class="more-icon">

             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="3417531">
            <b>
             2. Timber-step path.
            </b>
            Chunky pieces of wood create low steps in this curving gravel path. Flexible metal edging helps keep the stone from migrating into the surrounding beds.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="3913136" objid="3913136" posid="2">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/3913136/Lancaster-woodland-home-and-garden-traditional-landscape-philadelphia">
            <div>
             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by Hanselman Landscape and Gardens" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="562" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'3913136');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/056118f30192b6ba_1514-w422-h562-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/3913136/Lancaster-woodland-home-and-garden-traditional-landscape-philadelphia">

           </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="3913136">
            <b>
             3. Woodland path.
            </b>
            Fragrant pine-needle mulch surrounds round stepping stones leading to a bamboo and stone fountain in this wooded garden. A pathway like this is a wonderful way to bring a bit of magic to a shady spot in the yard.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="56004" objid="56004" posid="3">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/56004/Garden-design-contemporary-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>
             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by gytis" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'56004');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/3a415ff30c0cf1e2_2323-w606-h454-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/56004/Garden-design-contemporary-landscape-other-metro">

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="56004">
            <b>
             4. Stone-river path.
            </b>
            An intricate design like this one is as much a pleasure to look at as it is to walk on. A meandering stream of stones winding through a secondary bed of gravel invites exploration.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="742325" objid="742325" posid="4">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,496" data-stnd="606,403">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742325/Oakland-Residence-industrial-landscape-san-francisco">

             <img alt="Industrial Landscape by Thuilot Associates" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="403" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'742325');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/ea314d920f74e87d_2049-w606-h403-b0-p0--industrial-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742325/Oakland-Residence-industrial-landscape-san-francisco">

           </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="742325">
            <b>
             5. Birds-eye path.
            </b>
            Have a good view of your garden from an upstairs window? Make the most of it by designing a path that creates a distinct pattern when viewed from above. Here, a sharply angled gravel path leads into a circular seating area with a fire pit in the center.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="1599935" objid="1599935" posid="5">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">

             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by GEL: Griffin Enright Landscape" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'1599935');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/00c169040058cfc3_1910-w606-h454-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/1599935/Sunset-Residence-contemporary-landscape-los-angeles">

             </span>
            </a>
           </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="1599935">
            <b>
             6. Waves-of-grass path.
            </b>
            Steel risers form the base of this unique set of garden steps; Korean grass planted on top needs no mowing. This pathway begs to be experienced with bare feet!
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="365821" objid="365821" posid="6">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/365821/San-Marino-Residence-contemporary-landscape-los-angeles">
            <div>
             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by Ron Yeo, FAIA Architect" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="634" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'365821');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/6331d0460ec2edea_2160-w422-h634-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/365821/San-Marino-Residence-contemporary-landscape-los-angeles">

             </span>

           </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="365821">
            <b>
             7. Stone-step path.
            </b>
            Large stone slabs make up this striking path, which transitions seamlessly from steps to flat walking path. The organic, uneven edges of the broken stone look right at home under the cover of the redwood trees.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="19869795" objid="19869795" posid="7">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/19869795/Paths-and-Walkways-traditional-landscape-new-york">
            <div>
             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by Oliver Nurseries" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="634" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'19869795');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/3d21301f04627480_0962-w422-h634-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/19869795/Paths-and-Walkways-traditional-landscape-new-york">

           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="19869795">
            <b>
             8. Winding gravel path.
            </b>
            Curving paths can make a small garden feel larger. Enhance this space-expanding effect by using smaller plants in the foreground and taller, fuller plants and trees near the back of your path.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="624693" objid="624693" posid="8">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,496" data-stnd="606,403">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/624693/Belmont-Hill-Residence-contemporary-landscape-boston">

            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/624693/Belmont-Hill-Residence-contemporary-landscape-boston">

           </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="624693">
            <b>
             9. Gravel-look path.
            </b>
            To get the look of loose gravel without having to bother with scattering stones, the landscape designer of this space used a treatment called chip seal. Applied to asphalt, it creates the look and feel of loose gravel.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="1748898" objid="1748898" posid="9">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,496" data-stnd="606,403">

            </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="1748898">
            <b>
             10. Tree-lined lane.
            </b>
            If you have a large space to work with, consider a lane designed for strolling. Here, gravel is edged with bluestone, and the path is lined with Red Spire pear trees.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="16829951" objid="16829951" posid="10">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">

            <div>

            </div>

           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="16829951">
            <b>
             11. Stepping stone path.
            </b>
            Break up an expanse of lawn or make a narrow side lot feel special with the addition of a stepping stone path. Using various size stones and offsetting them (rather than lining them up perfectly) makes for a more relaxed, wild vibe.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="66023" objid="66023" posid="11">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/66023/Grass-Terraces-contemporary-landscape-san-francisco">
            <div>
             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by Shades Of Green Landscape Architecture" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="608" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'66023');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/c321e87f0c6ed9a2_2339-w422-h608-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="66023">
            <b>
             12. Modern path.
            </b>
            Complement modern architecture by repeating the lines and shapes of the structure in the paths that surround it. Here, square concrete pavers are set over river stones for a pleasingly geometric look.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="159756" objid="159756" posid="12">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/159756/Stanford-Garden-traditional-landscape-san-francisco">

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="159756">
            <b>
             13. Japanese garden path.
            </b>
            Stepping stones and a tiny bridge invite wandering and exploring in this Japanese-style garden. River stones act as the water beneath the bridge, and large stepping stones as islands.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>

             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by Statile &amp; Todd" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="403" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'798750');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/bae1de1f0f85d448_2066-w606-h403-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/798750/Garden-Path-traditional-landscape-newark">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/statiletodd/statile-and-todd">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Statile &amp; Todd
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="798750">
            <b>
             14. Broken bluestone path.
            </b>
            Irregularly shaped bluestone set into a fine gravel mix is a classic combination that works with many garden styles. Creeping thyme fills in the gaps between stones, while perennials and shrubs were chosen to bloom at different points throughout the season, so there is always something delightful to look at along the path.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="16587366" objid="16587366" posid="14">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/16587366/Judges-House-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>
             <img alt="Landscape by Hendy Curzon Gardens Ltd" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="562" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'16587366');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/68911c740406036b_1003-w422-h562-b0-p0--landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/16587366/Judges-House-landscape-other-metro">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/hendycurzon/hendy-curzon-gardens-ltd">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Hendy Curzon Gardens Ltd
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="16587366">
            <b>
             15. Cobblestone path.
            </b>
            Calling to mind charming cottages and places on a historic register, cobblestone paths are wonderful when you want to create the feeling that a garden has been around for many years
            <span>
             <span>
              <span>
               <span>
                 even if its new.
                <br/>
               </span>
              </span>
             </span>
            </span>
            <br/>
            <b>
             Tell us:
            </b>
            Which garden path captures your imagination?
            <br/>
            <br/>
            <b>
             More:
            </b>

             3 Essential Elements of an Artful Garden Path
            </a>

            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/path/ls=5" target="_blank">
             Browse thousands of inspiring path design photos
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>

                At first i thought, 'wow, a pebble that stays in place!' But, I agree with quarks. I think if you want that kind of surface, you need a good edge to keep most of the stones in. Then you just deal with the inevitable overflow.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                Last summer we hired Green Roots Environmental Design to help us with the turf area of the yard. I have two dogs, cockers, who live nose to the ground, so anything we put on the ground has to be non-toxic.  They installed a pebble path with a polymer coating to hold it in place. The polymer is inert when dry.  Here is a photo of the path (it winds through the middle of the photo) taken just before the project was finished and the pebbles closeup.
                <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/bf42f3ec050456c6_9-7722/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/bf42f3ec050456c6_7-7722/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="mhersey" data-type="profile">

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               We also have a dog that likes to break the land speed chasing squirrels ! We had a professional landscaper put down a new walkway using natural stone from our farm and filling the spaces with polymeric stone dust. The stone dust has a putty texture when spayed with water until it hardens. It has some flexibility and it not supposed to crack like cement. We have just gone through a bitterly cold winter and as the snow melts away so far so good.
               <br/>
               Regarding chip seal...we have a summer home in New Brunswick and the secondary roads are done with it. Great for roads but I don't think I want stones and oil on my front lawn.
              </div>

            <span class="anchorPoint" id="c24">
            </span>
            <a class="hlAnchor" name="738776">
            </a>
            <div class="flag-message text-center">
             Thank you for reporting this comment.
             <a class="flag-comment-action colorLink unflag" href="javascript:">

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="raulporter" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/c583633b0394ecaf_0-8848/raulporter.jpg"/>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="raulporter" data-type="profile">
               raulporter
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I LOVE garden paths.  I am a LAZY green gardener.  We have a mature beech forest for our back yard.  We have taken strips of old carpet as weed barriers throughout.  Carpet is always a beige or neutral.  It is mulched and eventually molds over tree roots and stays put over steep grades.  It protects the tree roots, keeps it walkable when it is muddy everywhere else and eventually becomes indistinguishable from the earth.  So once it starts to take on that mossy hue it no longer needs mulching.  It helps prevent erosion and keeps carpet out of landfill.  We also use carpet when we plan for a garden bed down the road.  The carpet is laid over what ever is growing and mulched.  The following year everything is dead below and the earthworms have moved in.  Water flows into the soil but weeds are prevented.  Then you just need to keep fall leaves off with a rake or blower....easy peasy.  The barrier cloth you can purchase is not as heavy duty.  Not for everyone, but like I said I'm lazy, cheap and green.
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="commentInfo tiny-text">

             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="cvaia" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/64b3a5ac0105677d_0-8267/cvaia.jpg"/>
             <div class="hzProIndicator text-center text-bold text-xs-xsmall">
              PRO
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="cvaia" data-type="profile">
               GreenThumb Gardens
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                Like any garden element, there is some maintenance for any feature. Any path will require a bit of care.  Pea Gravel paths are wonderful on the feet, eyes and ears, but do have to be laid over yard cloth, 'weeded' at least once a year, and cleaned of leaves and debris.  Stepping Stones in the lawn need to be imbedded in the sod, and leveled at install, and edged a few times a year, but can be mowed right over the rest of the time.   Wooden pavers and stone in shady areas may grow algae or moss on them and become slippery.
               </p>
               <p>
                Be careful with use of polmeric dust as it an inhalation danger while it is still dust.  After it sets, it it supposed to be impervious to water, so it, and any pavers or stones it surrounds are glued into place and are not water permeable.  This type of path, and chip seal, are somewhat permanent. (Think concrete)
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="alabamagal" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>

            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="alabamagal" data-type="profile">
               Margery
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                Lovely article!
               </p>
               <p>
                Pea gravel and river rock should NEVER be used for a 'walking surface' pretty as they are. That lovable round surface guarantees they will ALWAYS move, never ever settle down to become a firm surface. Wonderful for areas where walking is not an issue and perfect for 'streams'. Some version of crushed stone, concrete or shell is your option for a walking surface that is available in all 'grades' from fine to coarse. These finer ones can be compacted enough for wheelchair access. Chip and seal is what secondary roads were paved with way back in the 40s when I lived in Cincinnati. Used for REsurfacing  where the existing road was coated with liquid tar then followed up while hot with crushed stone. It is not porous but I can see its possibility in a limited walkway use. It eventually looks like a tar surface as the stone sinks in. Used now also in some states for main expressways.
               </p>
               <p>
                Any of the loose gravel surfaces WILL be tracked inside on the bottom of shoes and this can be an issue if you're lucky enough to have heart of pine floors like you may see here in AL.
               </p>
               <p>
                I love each of the above featured spaces for various reasons. Not a lover of formal settings in nature but the more natural ones really appeal. I am gradually replacing ALL lawn areas with plantings and pray that the moss that has now taken over will withstand our hot summers. I have little to no sun being surrounded by live oaks in the front, and what I call 'dead' oaks in back (water oaks which are short lived by comparison and lack the graciousness of the 'live' ones) I am gradually establishing pathways and plantings in lieu of grass. I'm from the midwest via tropical FL so now living in the Mobile Bay area which has the most rainfall per year of anywhere in the US...is a challenge! We are now 'painted' with chartreuse pollen from the oaks and  longleaf pines so I must stay indoors. Just as the weather is nice!
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                Last year I hired a landscaper because I was getting run off when it rains from the neighbor next door as my property is lower than the neighbors yard.  To make a long story short this landscaper comes in and puts a plastic ground cover down and then proceeds to put the rock pebble down on top of the plastic.  First rainstorm washed all the rocks off the plastic and down to the street.   Excuse the pun money down the drain.  I guess sometimes if you want something done right you have to do it yourself.  I am finding this out more and more.  I thought this was no big deal for someone to do this but obviously for this guy he had no clue on what he was doing.
               </p>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="alabamagal" data-type="profile">
               Margery
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                My landscaper is here this week (she's from out of town) and I spoke to her about the plastic she'd used under the river rock 'stream' she installed for me.  We left the plastic. An earlier discussion on HOUZZ spoke to this problem saying to NEVER put plastic down as the rock will slide off the plastic. It does! I still get weeds in the rocks from the silt. rotted leaves that collect in the crevices. I think one answer might be to insist on using more and larger rocks. Though that's not as 'easy' it looks more natural. Personally I want the edges to be uneven, not edged as at least one of those pictured is with a neatly trimmed lawn abutting it. We get torrential rain storms here on Mobile Bay and the bigger the rocks the less washing. I had a larger 'stream' when I lived in Cincinnati and a severe storm never budged it. No plastic was used and a LOT of rocks of different sizes went into that stream, I mean some huge rocks that diverted the force adequately.
               </p>
              </div>

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="renorobin" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/31836a390e184061_0-2193/renorobin.jpg"/>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="renorobin" data-type="profile">
               renorobin
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                A comment in favour of using 1/2 inch river stone: I created a path on either side of my house, leading towards the back. Its natural texture provides a lovely visual effect with my contrasting shade garden plants on either side. In addition, it feels so good underfoot....and the soft crunch, crunch as one walks on it gives a pleasant auditory effect. A bonus: I am able to hear anyone arriving unexpectedly if I am working in the back garden or am on the deck. And as a safety plus, even from inside the house, I would be able to hear anyone walking on it.
               </p>
               <p>
                Of course, through trial and error, I understand the importance of using the thick,
                <b>
                 heavy duty
                </b>
                weed barrier underneath the paths to prevent grass or weeds from coming through...but organic debris from trees such as seeds, leaves in fall etc..do fall on it and decompose and we find that grass or the occasional weed will start to grow on top of it....but even after 6 years, usually just a quick scuff with the foot eliminates it. There is virtually no maintenance at all if weeding is done in the adjacent beds. I'd say I spend 10 minutes per summer pulling up the occasional, little stray weed. Because I lose some stones every fall when I rake up leaves, every spring I buy a bag of river stone to top up the paths. I love my river stone paths!
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="commentInfo tiny-text">

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="renorobin" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/31836a390e184061_0-2193/renorobin.jpg"/>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="renorobin" data-type="profile">
               renorobin
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                I have read previous posts about how small river stone gets brought into the house on the bottom of shoes and how it shifts underfoot. I just don't have that experience AT ALL...and I have 4 groups of around 8 people come into my house every week. Yes, the stone moves a bit underfoot which is a pleasant sensation..as well as the sound. I think if you put too many stones you could sink into it too much. Mine is about 1 inch deep max. The paths down both sides of the house are river stone and we enjoyed them so much that we created another area in the back where only moss grew under the trees. The vinca spreads out over it a bit on the sides to make the contours soft and uneven. The contrast is lovely. We put a metal bistro set that I spray painted white on it so it pops from a distance. The chair and table legs rest directly on the surface under the stones so that they are stable. Last year, we cleared another patch of grass 2 steps down from our sunny deck to create another smaller, shadier seating area surrounded by ferns. I have 4 large uneven beds that fill my whole garden except for paths of grass around them all...and I have even considered getting rid of ALL the grass and replacing it with river stone so that my four large beds are surrounded by river stone. That would be so pretty. I LOVE my river stone.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="cathy_edger" data-type="profile">
               Edger Landscape Design
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                I concur w/renorobin about the shallow depth gravel pathways. A proper compacted baserock foundation is essential and then a shallow decorative gravel layer make for simple construction and firm footing. Often the biggest challenge is not overdoing it with the gravel layer. Also, I would not use gravel larger than 3/4" in diameter on a pathway, unless there were also firm, generously sized stepping stones.
               </p>
              </div>

		window.hzmr.allResources = ["jquery-1.11.1","hz","hzConfig","hzI18n","hzLogger","hzMain","main","bmblock","errSt","jquery-migrate-1.2.1","jqUtils","core","ui","responsiveReload","autoSuggest","standardHeaderV6","CartActionsRequest","SetVisitorPropertyRequest","CommentManagerAjaxRequest","FeedAjaxRequest","ContentFlagAjaxRequest","LightboxGetNextSpacesRequest","LightboxGetSpaceDataRequest","GetMinimalSpaceDataRequest","GetManufacturerColorsRequest","SaveColorPaletteRequest","HoverCardRequest","FollowRequest","SendEmailRequest","CurrentSessionUserInfoRequest","LikeRequest","ClippingRequest","TopicAjaxRequest","TipBoxRequest","SearchSuggestionRequest","VerificationEmailRequest","NoOpRequest","BuzzRequest","BuzzAjaxRequest","GetEmbedSpaceCodeRequest","GetQuestionsForSpaceRequest","UserPreferencesRequest","TrustPilotAjaxRequest","AuthorizeRequest","FbUserRequest","JsErrorLoggerAjaxRequest","SearchKeywordRequest","GetSpaceAjaxRequest","spaceActions","socialSdk","siteAuth","postFbPageDialog","flashdetect","filterRenderer","hzGtm","scrollToTop","cartActions","signupDialog","jquery.history","jquery-ui-1.10.3.custom.widget-only","feedComments","highlightComment","jquery.form.min","lightbox","productVariation","lightboxAdPlugins","lightboxPlugins","returnPolicy","profileUtils","zoomable","spacePreview","colorPicker","colorAnalyzer","newPostSignup","hoverCard","followButton","fontSmoothing","contactDialog","widgetWizard","flagContent","like","clipping","jquery.visible","topics","ideabookInterstitial","tipBox","viewGallery"];
		for (var i=0; i&lt;window.hzmr.length; i++) {
			var filename = window.hzmr[i];
			var v = filename.split(':')[1];
			if (v != window.hzmr.version) {
				HZ.utils.Logger.sendError({msg:"Page version "+window.hzmr.version+" doesn't match file version "+filename, file:null, line:null, url:window.location.href});
				break;
			}
		}
		var missingFiles = [];
		var loadedResources = {};
		for (var i=0; i&lt;hzmr.length; i++) { loadedResources[hzmr[i].match(/(.*):\d*/)[1]] = true; }
		for (var i=0; i&lt;hzmr.allResources.length; i++) {
			key = hzmr.allResources[i];
			if (!loadedResources[key]) {
				missingFiles.push (key);
			}
		}
		if (missingFiles.length &gt; 0) {
			var msg = "JS resource failed to load: " + missingFiles[0] + ".";
			if (missingFiles.length &gt; 1) {
				msg += " Missing out on: " + missingFiles.slice(1).join(",") + ".";
			}
			HZ.utils.Logger.sendError({msg:msg, file:null, line:null, url:window.location.href});
		}
	}
})();
   </script>
